44.     Continuous Functions on Compact Metric Spaces

Continuous functions on compact metric spaces have many nice properties.
We have already proved (Corollary 42.7) that a continuous real-valued
function on a compact metric space attains a maximum and a minimum.
In this section we will derive further results.

Theorem44.1     If f is a continuous function from a compact metric space
M 1 into a metric space M 2 , then/(M 1) is compact.
44.   Continuous Functions on Compact Metric Spaces                         163

  Proof. Let {f(x,J} be a sequence in /(M 1). Then {x11} is a sequence in
Mi, and since M 1 is compact, {x11} has a subsequence {x11.,} which converges
to a in M 1 (Theorem 43.5). Since/is continuous at a, liIDt...00 /(x 11.,) =/(a).
Therefore {/(x 11.,)} is a convergent subsequence of {/(x,J} in M 2 • By Theorem
43.5,f(M 1) is compact. ■

Corollary44.2 If/ is a continuous function from a compact metric space
M 1 into a metric space M 2 , then/(M 1) is closed and bounded.
  Proof.     The proof follows immediately from Theorems 43.7 and 44.1.        ■


  The next theorem shows that a one-to-one continuous function on a com-
pact metric space has a continuous inverse.

1beorem44.3 If / is a one-to-one continuous function from a compact
metric space M 1 onto a metric space M 2 , then/- 1 is continuous on M 2 •

argument can be modified to show that/- 1{x) = x 11" is continuous on R.
   Let/be a continuous function from a metric space (M 1, d1) into a metric

d 1(x, y) < 6, then d2 (/(x),f(y)) < 8. In general, 6 will depend upon y.

uniformly continuous on M.

45. Connected Metric Spaces

Under any reasonable definition of connectedness, the metric space X =
[O, I] u [2, 3] should not be connected. Notice that [O, I] is open and closed
in X (verify) and [O, I] is neither X nor 0- By Theorems 38.4 and 39.3, if M


                                                                               ....
 168                                                            Metric Spaces

 is a metric space, then M and 0 are both open and closed. If these are the
 only subsets of M which are both open and closed, we say that M is con-
 nected.

 Definition45.1 Let M be a metric space. If the only subsets of M which are
 both open and closed are Mand     0, then we say that Mis connected.
   To prove that a metric space is connected, we often argue by contradiction,
 and so it is convenient to have a characterization of "not connected."

1beorem45.2 Let M be a metric space. The following are equivalent.
  (i) M is not connected.
 (ii) There exist open nonempty subsets U and V of M such that M = U u V
      and Un V= 0.
(iii) There exist closed nonempty subsets C and D of M such that M = C u D
      and Cn D = 0.
   Proof. Suppose M is not connected. Then there exists an open and closed
subset U of M such that U is neither M nor 0. If we let V = U', then Vis
open and V ¢ 0. Therefore M = U u V, Un V = 0, and U and Vare
open and nonempty.
   Now suppose that there exist open nonempty subsets U and V of M such
that M = U u V and Un V = 0. Since U' = V, U is closed. Thus U is
an open and closed subset of M which is neither M nor 0, and hence M
is not connected. Therefore parts (i) and (ii) are equivalent.
   The equivalence of parts (i) and (iii) is proved in a similar way. ■

  We may characterize the connected subsets of the real line using the next
theorem.

Theorem45.3 A subset X of R is connected if and only if whenever a,b e X
with a < b, we have [a, b] c X.
   Proof. Let X be a connected subset of R. Suppose there exist a,b e X
with a < b, but c ¢ X for some c e [a, b]. Then U = {x e XI x < c} and
 V = {x e XI c < x} are open nonempty subsets of X such that X = U u V
and Un V = 0. By Theorem 45.2, Xis not connected, and we have a con-
tradiction. Thus whenever a,b e X with a < b, we have [a, b] c X.
   Now suppose that whenever a,b e X with a < b, we have [a, b] c X. If X
is not connected, by Theorem 45.2, there exist closed nonempty subsets C
and D of X such that X = Cu D and C n D = 0- Let a e C and be D.
We may assume that a< b. Let
                           c = lub {x e CI x < b}
46. Connected Metric Spaces                                                167


For every positive integer n, there exists x,. e C such that c - 1/n < x,. s c.
Thus lim,... x,. = c, and c is a limit point of C. Since C is closed, c e C.
            <X)


Now c <band by our hypothesis, (c, b] c X.
  We next show that (c, b) c D. If there exists ye (c, b) such that y ¢ D,
then ye C. Now y <bandy e C; soy e {x e CI x < b}. Thus
                        y S c = lub {x e CI x < b}
Since ye (c, b), y > c which is impossible, and thus (c, b) c D.
  Because (c, b) c D, c is a limit point of D, and since D is closed, c e D.
But now c e C ri D = 0, which is a contradiction, and therefore X is
connected. ■

Corollary45.4 A nonempty subset X of R is connected if and only if X
is either a point or an interval. In particular, R is connected.
  Proof.     Points and intervals are connected by Theorem 45.3.
   Now suppose that X is a connected subset of R. Let a = glb X and b =
lub X. (We let a= - oo if Xis not bounded below, and we let b = oo if X
is not bounded above). If a = b, then X = {a}; so suppose a < b. Let
c e (a, b). There exist x and yin X such that a < x < c < y < b. By Theorem
45.3, c e X. Thus (a, b) c X. If x < a or if x > b, then x ¢ X. Therefore X
is one of (a, b), (a, b), (a, b], or (a, b]. ■

  The next theorem is identical to Theorem 44.1 with compact replaced by
coMected.

46. Complete Metric Spaces

We define a Cauchy sequence in a metric space analogously to Definition
19.2 which applies to real sequences.

Definition46.1 Let (M, d) be a metric space. A sequence {x,.} in M is a
Cauchy sequence if for every e > 0, there exists a positive integer N such that
if m,n ~   N, then d(x,,., x,.) < e.

   As in the case of the real line, every convergent sequence in a metric space
is a Cauchy sequence.

1beorem46.2 If {x,.} is a convergent sequence in a metric space, then {x,.}
is a Cauchy sequence.
  Proof. Let {x,.} be a convergent sequence in a metric space (M, d).
Suppose lim,.... x,. = x. Let e > 0. There exists a positive integer N such
                 Cll


that if n ~ N, then d(x,., x) < e/2. If m,n ~ N, then


                                                                                •
180                                                                Metric Spaces

  The converse of Theorem 46.2 holds for the real line (l'heor~m 19.3);
however, the converse of Theorem 46.2 does not hold for an arbitrary metric
space. For example, the sequence {1/n}:-'.,1 is a Cauchy sequence in (0, 2),
but {l/n}:= 1 does not converge in (0, 2). If the converse of Theorem 46.2
holds for a metric space M, M is said to be complete.

Definition46.3 Let M be a metric space. If every Cauchy sequence in M
is convergent, we say that M is a complete metric space.

  Thus R is a complete metric space, but (0, 2) is not a complete metric
space.
  If Xis a set with the discrete metric d, Xis complete. For let {x,.} be a
Cauchy sequence in X. There exists a positive integer N such that if m,n ~ N,
then d(x.,, x,.) < ½-Therefore, if n ~ N, x,. = xN, and thus {x,.} converges
to XN•

Example46.8 Let us apply our results from Theorem 46. 7 to the metric
space (M, d) = (Q, d), where Q is the set of rational numbers and d(x, y) =
48. Complete Metric Spaces                                                            186

 Ix - yl for all x,y e Q is the usual Euclidean distance. Now Mi will be the
collection of all equivalence classes of Cauchy sequences of rational numbers.

   The mapping ll: Q-+ Mi is, for example, ll(t) = X, where Xis the set
of all Cauchy sequences of rational numbers which converge to ½.Note that
(½,½,½,... ) is one such sequence.
   (Mi, di) can be shown to satisfy Definition 3.2. That is, Mi = R. Thus
the proof of Theorem 46.7 can be used to construct the real numbers from
the rational numbers.

47.    Baire Category Theorem

We begin with a definition.

Deflnidon 47.3 Let M be a metric space and let X be a subset of M. We
say that X is nowheredense (in M) if x-' - = M.




                                                                                                                                  ......
168                                                             Metric Spaces

   For example, a point of R is nowhere dense in R. However, a point of P
is not nowhere dense in P. These examples show that the concept of a set X
being nowhere dense is defined only relative to a metric space of which X
is a subset. This situation is different from such concepts as compactness or
connectedness. A set X is compact or connected in and of itself. It is im-
material in which metric space it resides.
   Before giving the next definition we note that a closed set X is nowhere
dense in M if its complement is dense in M.

Definition47.4 Let M be a metric space and let X be a subset of M. We
say that X is of first category (in M) if X is a countable union of nowhere
dense sets. We say that Xis of second category (in M) if Xis not of first
category (in M).
   Just as the definition of X being nowhere dense is relative to the metric
space M of which X is a subset, so too are the concepts of first and second
category relative to the containing metric space M.
   It is easy to see that a countable union of sets of first category (in M)
is of first category (in M). We pointed out before that a point is nowhere
dense in R, and thus any countable subset of R is of first category in R. In
particular, the set of rational numbers is of first category in R. If the set of
irrational numbers were of first category in R, then R = Q u Q' would be
of first category. This is not, however, the case as the Daire category theorem
shows.

Theorem47.5 (DaireCategoryTheorem) If M is a nonempty complete
metric space, then M is of second category (in M).
  Proof. Suppose that M is of first category. Then M = U:°-             1 A,.,
where each A,. is nowhere dense in M. Now M = U:'-1 A; and thus 0 =
n:.  I (A;'). Now each set A; I is an open dense subset of M; so by Theorem
47.2, 0 =   n::  1 (A;') is dense in M. This contradiction establishes the
theorem. ■

   The Daire category theorem has many applications in analysis. The usual
application is to show that a point x of a complete metric space exists with
a particular property P. A typical argument runs as follows. Let X = {x e
MI x does not have property P}. By some argument, we show that Xis of
first category. Since Mis of second category, there exists x e Mn X'. Thus
x has property P.
   We give one interesting application of the Baire category theorem.

Definition47.6 Let M be a metric space and let x e M. We say that xis an
isolated point of M if for some e > 0, B,(x) n M = {x}.
47. Bair• Category Theorem                                                     189

1beorem47.7 If Mis a complete metric space with no isolated points, then
M is uncountable.
  Proof Suppose Mis countable. Then M =              U:.
                                                    1 {a,.}, and each set is
nowhere dense, since M has no isolated points. Thus Mis of first category,
and this contradicts Theorem 47.5. ■

Corollary47.8 If Mis a compact metric space with no isolated points, then
M is uncountable.
  Proof     A compact metric space is complete. Now apply Theorem 47.7.          ■

    If /is differentiable for all points in (a, b), then by investigating the behavior

Theorem49.5 If f and g are continuous real functions on [a, b] which are
differentiable in (a, b), then there is a point c e (a, b) at which
                     [f(b) - /(a)]•g'(c)   = [g(b) - g(a)],f'(c)                (49.3)
   Proof.   Define
      h(x) = [f(b) - f(a)]g(x) - [g(b) - g(a)]f(x)            for all x e [a, b].
Then his continuous on [a, b] and differentiable in (a, b) and
                        h(a) ==f(b)g(a) - f(a)g(b)     = h(b)                   (49.4)
All we need is that there exists c e (a, b) such that h'(c) = 0, for then
              0 = h'(c) = [f(b) - f(a)]g'(c) - [g(b) - g(a)]f'(c)

If his a constant function, then h'(c) = 0 for all c in (a, b), which completes
the proof.
   Suppose h(t) > h(a) for some t in (a, b). By Theorem 34.4, h attains a
maximum at some c in [a, b]. But by (49.4), since h(c) ~ h(t) > h(a), we
must have c e (a, b); so by Theorem 49.3, h'(c) ==0.
   If h(t) < h(a) for some t in (a, b) a similar argument gives us a c e (a, b)
with h'(c) -= 0. ■

   The above theorem is often called the generalized mean-value theorem.

50. Taylor's Theorem

 In this section we look at the problem of approximating a real-valued function
/by a polynomial function P. That is, given/ on (a, b), find a polynomial P

/ where it is impossible to find P as above, but we shall develop a method
 applying to certain types of functions/, which produces a polynomial P, where
 we have some information about the accuracy of the approximation.

The Riemann integral solves the problem of defining the area under the graph

  What we have done is average the function/ over an interval [a, b] weighted

generalizes this process by allowing weights other than length. This more
general integral, which includes the Riemann integral as a special case, has
many applications as, for example, in the theory of probability.

for any points T. Thus if the upper and lower sums approximate the integral,
we would expect that the Riemann-Stieltjes sums would also approximate
the integral. The next theorem shows that this is the case.

   The condition "P* =>P" in Theorem 52.2 is analogous to the condition
"n ~ N" in the definition of the limit of a sequence. These concepts can be
treated under a unified theory, the theory of directed sets or filters. (See
Kelly, 1955, Chapter 2.)
   Theorem 52.2 shows that refining partitions is a way of forcing convergence
of Riemann-Stieltjes sums. Another way to describe convergence of Riemann-
Stieltjes sums is given in the next definition.

Deftnitioa52.3 Let P = {x0 , x 1 , •••              , x,.} be a partition   of [a, b]. We define
                  norm P = max {Ix, - x,-il: 1 sis                       n}
Let/ and (X be bounded functions on (a, b]. We say that
                                Jim            S(f, P, T) = I
                            norm p ..      o
if for every s > 0, there exists 6 > 0 such that for any partition P of [a, b]
with norm P < 6 and for any points T, we have
                                IS(f, P, T) - II < e
   We cannot prove a theorem analogous to Theorem 52.2 for the kind of
convergence described in Definition 52.3. That is, convergence of Riemann-
Stieltjes sums as described in Definition 52.3 is not equivalent to the existence
of the Riemann-Stieltjes integral. For example, let
                                                   if-lsx<O
                                                   ifOsxsl

and
                                                   ifO<xSl
62. Rlemann-StleltjN Suma                                                       207

Let P = {-1, 0, l}. Then U(J, P) = L(J, P) = l, and thus f e &t.[-1, l].
However, limnormp ... 0 S(J, P,      n

54.   Functions of Bounded Variation

In this section we define bowided variation. Functions of bounded variation
are close relatives of increasing functions (Theorem 54.9) and the results
of this section will be used to extend the results of Section 51.

            51.13 to prove that if«, Pe BV[a, b], then «· P,lotl,and 1/P(if P is

58. The Riemann Integral

change of variable formula for the Riemann integral.

57. Measure Zero

The next two sections of this chapter are devoted to deriving a necessary and
sufficient condition for the existence of the Riemann integral. We will prove

Definition57.1 Let X be a subset of R. We say that Xis of measurezero if

that U:'.1 / 11 => X and I::',.1 11,.1

interval I.

  In other words, a subset X of R is of measure zero if X can be covered by a
countable family of open intervals the sum of whose lengths can be made
arbitrarily small.

69.   Improper Riemann-Stieltjes Integrals

So far in this chapter, all of our functions have been bounded and all of our
integrals have been computed over closed, bounded intervals. In this section
we relax these restrictions by defining improper Riemann-Stieltjes integrals.

Deftnition59.1 Let f and a be functions defined on the interval [a, oo).

function s of Definition 22.1. The function F is sometimes called the partial
integral in analogy with the partial sum of an infinite series. Convergence
of an improper integral is defined in terms of convergence of the partial
integral F(b) = I!f da.

                  Sequences and Series of Functions




In this chapter we shall investigate convergence of sequences and series of
functions. The results of this chapter will extend the results of Chapters IV
and V. In particular, we shall be concerned with the following problem. If
each element of a sequence {!..} of functions has a particular property, when
does the limit function/ have the same property? For example, we might
ask whether the limit function/ is continuous if each function/.. is continuous.
We begin by defining convergence of a sequence of functions.


80.   Pointwise Convergence and Uniform Convergence

We shall be concerned with sequences of real-valued functions defined on
some set X. We shall denote a sequence as {f..}:. 1 (or sometimes just {f..}).
Thus for each positive integer n we have a real-valued function/.. defined on a
set X. The set X will not, in general, be a subset of the real numbers.

 Definition60.1 Let {!..} be a sequence of functions on a set X. Let / be a
 function on X. We say that{!..} convergespointwiseto/ on Xiflm,. ➔ 0 f.(x) =
/(x) for every x in X.

Example60.2 Let
                          f..(x) = x'
Then {!..} converges pointwise to/ on [O, 1), where
                                         Osx<l
                          /(x)   = {~
                                         X   =}

                                                                      246
248                                         Sequencn end Serln of Function•

   Notice that in Example 60.2 each function/. is continuous on (0, 1) but
that the pointwise limit function/is not continuous on [O,1). Thus continuity
is not in general preserved under taking pointwise limits. Thus if we wish to
preserve continuity we must seek a stronger definition of convergence of a
sequence of functions. One of the most useful notions is that of uniform
convergence. (See Theorems 60.4 and 61.1.)

Definition60.3 Let {/.} be a sequence of functions on a set X. Let / be a
function on X. We say that {/.} converges uniformly to/ on X if for every
a > 0, there exists a positive integer N such that if n ~ N, then
                               IJ.(x) - /(x)I < a
for every x in X.

   The crucial part of Definition 60.3 is that 1/.(x) - /(x)I < e for every x
in X (if n ~ N). That is, N depends on a, but not on x.
   It is clear that if a sequence of functions converges uniformly it also
converges pointwise (verify).
   We now prove that continuity is preserved under uniform convergence.

83. Applications to Power Series

We recall that a power series is a series of the form I::°-oa.(x - t)" (see
Sections 27 and 29). If R is the radius of convergence of a power series

and diverges if Ix - t I > R (see Section 27). We will use the results of Section

term for Ix - ti < R.

85. Summability Methods and Tauberian Theorems

Consider the divergent series I:~o (-1)•.        If lxl < I, then I::. 0 (- l)"X-
converges and
                     00              1
                       L <-•rx-
                       ••0
                             = -,1 + X              lxl< I
Since limx ➔ l 1/{l + x) = ½.we can assign I::..o(- 1r the generalized sum
of;. This summability method is known as Abel summability.

Definition65.1 If I::.o a. is an infinite series such that I;,:0.0 a.x"converges
if lxl < I and limx ➔ 1 I::°-0 a.X- = T exists, we say that I:,:O.   0 a. is Abel
summable (to n.

  By Corollary 64.3, if I:.:°=o
                              a,. converges to L, then the Abel sum and the
ordinary sum (L = I::°-oa,.) are the same. Thus Abel summability gen-
eralizes the ordinary method of summing an infinite series (Definition 22.2).
  Another generalized sum is the Cesaro sum.

Definition65.2 Let I::°-1 a. be an infinite series. Let {s.} be the sequence
of partial sums of the series I::°-1 a•. If lim. ➔ 0 (s 1 + s 2 + · · · + s.)/n = T
exists, we say that I::°-1 a. is Cesaro summable (to    n.
   By Theorem 20.7, if I::°-1 a. converges to L, then the Cesaro sum and the
ordinary sum (L = I::°-1 a.) are the same. The divergent series I::°-o(- 1r
has Cesaro sum ½(verify), and thus Cesaro summability also generalizes
the ordinary method of summing an infinite series. Cesaro summability plays
an important role in the theory of Fourier series (see Chapter XIO.
   It can be shown that if I::°-oa. is Cesaro summable to T, then          I::. 0 a,.
is Abel summable to T, but that there are Abel summable series which are
not Cesaro summable (see Exercises 65.1 and 65.2).
   Theorem 64.3 can be rephrased as follows: If I:,:O.         0 a. converges to L,
then I;,:0.0 a,. is Abel summable to L. The series I:.ao.   0 (-tr   shows that the
converse of this theorem is false. If certain additional hypotheses are imposed,
it is often possible to deduce that I:.00• 0 a,.converges. Such a theorem is called
a Tauberian theorem and the additional hypotheses are called Tauberian
conditions. There are very complicated Tauberian theorems which play an
important role in higher analysis. We shall give one example now of a
Tauberian theorem and another later (Theorem 79.1). Other Tauberian
theorems are given as exercises (Exercises 65.3 and 65.4).

      XII
Inner Product Spaces and Fourier Series




In many of the metric spaces we have studied it is possible to define addition
and scalar multiplication in such a way that the spaces become vector spaces
over the field of real numbers. (Appendix: Vector Spaces summarius the
definition of a vector space and some elementary results we will be using
subsequently). For example, if we define addition and scalar multiplication
pointwise in R•, / 2 , and lf[a, b], each of these sets becomes a vector space
over R. Thus these spaces (and others) have a much richer structure than
merely being equipped with a distance function. In R• it is possible to define
the length or norm of a vector which corresponds to the geometric interpreta-
tion of length. It is possible to generalii.e the concept of the length of a vector
to / 2 and lf[a, b] (and to other spaces as well). A vector space with a length
function is called a normed linear space. The vector space R• has another
important concept associated with it and that is the idea of perpendicularity
or orthogonality of pairs of vectors in R•. This concept also generalius to / 2
and lf[a, b]. A vector space with an orthogonality-measuring function is
called an inner product space. In this chapter we will study inner product
spaces and orthogonal expansion of vectors in inner product spaces with
particular attention to lf[a, a + 2n], where a e R.


69. Normed Linear Spaces

Ifwe define
            (xi, ... , x,.) + (yi, ... , y,.) = (x 1 + y 1, •••        ,   x,. + y,.)
                         c(x 1, •••   ,   x,.) = (cxi, ...   , ex,.)

      280
19. Normed UnHr Spacu                                                            281

where (x., ... , x.), (y 1, ••• , y.) e R• and c e R, then R• becomes a real
vector space. The i.cro vector of R• is the n-tuple (0, 0, ... , 0) and the
additive inverse of (x 1, ••• , xJ is ( - x 1 , ••• , - xJ. It is easy to verify that
the other vector space axioms are also satisfied. We shall always denote the
zero vector of a vector space by 8; this is to avoid confusion with the real
number 0.
   In R• it is possible to define an "absolute-value-type" function. We let

                             Hxll =   Jx~+ · · · + x!                         (69.1)
where x = (x., ... , x.) e R•. If n = l, llxll is precisely lxl, the absolute
value of x. We call llxll the norm of x. Geometrically, the norm of x is the
length of the vector x. This norm function satisfies the following properties:
  (i) llxll = 0 if and only if x = 8.
 (ii) llcxll= lcl llxll for c e R, x e R•.
(iii) llx + YIIS llxll + IIYII  for x, ye R•.
Properties (i) and (ii) are easily verified. Property (iii) follows from the
triangle inequality for the metric space R•. Indeed, if d denotes the usual
metric for R• and x = (x., ... , x.), y = (y 1 , ••• , yJ e R•, we have

                  llx + YII=   Jex.+ Y1)2 + •· · + (x.+ yJ2
                            = d(x, -y)
                            s d(x, 0) + d(O, - y)
                            = J x~+ •.. + x! + J y~ + .. · + Y!
                            = llxll + IIYII
  The preceding discussion motivates the next definition. We remark at this
point that all vector spaces in this text are over the field of real numbers.

Deftnition69.1 Let V be a vector space. A norm for V is a function II· II
from V into [O,oo) which satisfies the following:
  (i) llxll = 0 if and only if x = 0.
 (ii) llcxll = lcl llxll force R, x e V.
(iii) llx + YIIS llxll + IIYII for x, ye V.
The ordered pair (V, II·II)(or more simply, V) is called a normedlinearspace
(or normed vectorspace).

  Property (iii) of Definition 69.1 is called the triangleinequality.
  We have already observed that equation (69.1) defines a norm on R•. To
give further examples of normed linear spaces, we must first tum some of our
familiar examples into vector spaces.

Theorem69.3 If ( V, II· II)is a normed linear space, then the equation

by Definition 69.l(i) holds if and only if x - y = 0. Thus property (i) of

  Whenever we apply any metric space definitions (such as continuity, limit
of a sequence, open set, compact, complete, etc.) to a normed linear space V,
unless otherwise specified, it will be assumed that the metric for V is that
given in Theorem 69.3. For example, a sequence {x,,} in a normed linear
space V converges to x in V if for every s > 0, there exists a positive integer
N such that if n ~ N, we have

                                  llx,. - xii = d(x,,, x) < e
  A complete normed linear space is called a Banach space. Thus each of the
spaces R",/ 1, / 2 , and C[a, b] are Banach spaces. (See Section 46 and Corollary
60.8.)
  Normed linear spaces will be considered in some detail in Chapter XIII.
In the remainder of this chapter we will be concerned with a special kind of
normed linear space known as an inner product space.

70. The Inner Product Space R3

In R3 one encounters the innerproduct (or dot product) defined by the equation
                          (x, y) = X1Y1 + X2Y2 + X3y3
where x = (x 1, x 2, x 3), y = (y 1, y 2, y 3) e R3 • The norm of a vector x in R 3
is related to the inner product by the equation
                                 Hxll= J(x, x)
    Among the properties of the inner product useful in computations (all of
which are easily verified) are
   (i) (ex + y, z) = c(x, z) + (y, z) for all c e Rand x, y, z e R3 •
  (ii) (x, cy + z) = c(x, y) + (x, z) for all c e Rand x, y, z e R3 •
(iii) (x, y) = (y, x) for all x, ye R3 •
 (iv) (x, x) = 0 if and only if x = 0.
    Properties (i) and (ii) state that the inner product is linear in each coor-
dinate.
    If x and y are unit vectors in R3 , that is, if Hxll= 1 = IIYII,the inner
product (x, y) has the geometric interpretation as cos q,,where q,is the angle
between x and y (see Figure 70.1). The inner product gives a useful test for
perpendicularity. The nonzero vectors x and yin R 3 are perpendicular if and
only if cos q,= 0, where q,is the angle between x and y. Thus unit vectors x
and y are perpendicular if and only if (x, y) = 0. Arbitrary nonzero vectors
x and y are perpendicular if and only if the associated unit vectors x/llxll and
y/llyll are perpendicular. Thus nonzero vectors x and y are perpendicular if
and only if (x/llxll, y/llyll) = 0. This equation holds (using the linearity of
the inner product) if and only if (x, y) = 0. Thus nonzero vectors in R3 are
perpendicular if and only if (x, y) = 0. If we adopt the standard assumption
that any vector is perpendicular to the zero vector, we have the following
theorem: vectors x and y are perpendicular in R 3 if and only if (x, y) = 0.
We will use the term orthogonalinterchangably with perpendicular.




                                    Figura 70.1
288                                         Inner Product Spaces end Fourier Series




                                  Figure 70.2

72. Orthogonal Seta in Inner Product Spaces

We begin by defining orthogonality in abstract inner product spaces in
terms of the inner product. (See Section 70 for motivation.)

Deflnidon72.1 Let V be an inner product space. We say that vectors x and
y in V are orthogonal(or perpendicular)if (x, y) = 0. A set X of vectors in V
is said to be an orthogonalset if any two distinct vectors in X are orthogonal.
A set X of vectors in V is said to be an orthonormalset if Xis an orthogonal
set for which llxll = 1 for every x e X.

73.    Periodic Functions

In this section we will first justify changing the limits of integration in the
integral of a periodic function (Theorem 73.2). This fact was used in the
proof of Theorem 72.2(iii). We will then briefly examine the problem of
extending a given function to a periodic function.

 ± 2, .... (See Figure 73.2.)
   We will let CP[a, b] denote the set of functions continuous on R and
periodic of period b - a. Our above discussion shows that CP[a, b] can be
identified with the set of continuous functions/on [a, b] satisfying/(a) = f(b).
The set CP[a, b] plays an important role in the theory of the inner product
space Bt[a, b]. (See Section 77.)

   Let {x., x 2 , ••• } be an orthonormal set in an inner product space Vand
let x e V. If we ask whether the Fourier series of x converges to x, we are
asking whether the series
                                          co
                                         L (x, x.)x,.
                                         ••1

converges to x. In an arbitrary inner product space, the norm is given by
IIYII= ✓ (Y, y), y e V, and thus we are asking if the sequence of partial sums
of the series I::'-1 (x, x,.)x,. converges to x in the inner product space norm.
Therefore, the Fourier series of x converges toxin the inner product space
norm if for every e > 0, there exists a positive integer N such that if k ~ N,
we have

In the inner product space Bl[a,a + 2n] convergence of the Fourier series

                                                                 <e

We have called such convergence L 2 -convergence. We will show (Theorem
78.5) that the Fourier series of a function f e Bl[a,a + 2n] converges to/ in
the L 2-sense.
   The sequence of partial sums of the Fourier series of a function f e Bl[a,
a + 21t] is a sequence of real-valued functions. Thus we can also pose the
questions: (1) Does the Fourier series of/ converge pointwise? (2) Does the
Fourier series of/ converge uniformly?
   Question ( 1) is very difficult. It has been known for some time that con-
tinuity off at x does not imply convergence of the Fourier series of/ at x.
(See Hewitt and Stromberg, 1965.) It has recently been shown that if/ e Bl[a,
a + 2n], then the Fourier series of/ converges almost everywhere to/.
74. Fourier Serl•: Definition• and Exampl•                                        301

(See Carleson, 1966.) We will show (Corollary 76.8) that if f is differentiable
at x, then the Fourier series of/ converges to fat x.
   Concerning question (2), we will prove (Theorem 77.5) that if f e CP[a,
a + 2n ], then the Fourier series off is uniformly Cesaro summable to f and
that if f e CP[a, a + 2n] n BV[a, a + 2n], then the Fourier series of /
converges uniformly to f (Theorem 79 .3).

78.    Fourier Series in at[a, a + 2n]

In this section we will extend certain of the results of the previous section to
the inner product space at[a, a + 21t].Such generalizations are possible be-
cause any function in ~[a, a + 2n] is L 2-approximable by a function in
CP[a, a + 2n] {Theorem 78.4). The main result of this section is Theorem
78.5 which states that the trigonometric set is complete in at[a, a + 21t].

79. A Tauberian Theorem and an Application to Fourier Series

Fejer's theorem (Theorem 77.5) states that the Fourier series of a function/
in CP[a, a + 2n] is uniformly Cesaro summable to f. Thus a Tauberian
theorem of the form, "If     :r:..
                              1 f,. is uniformly Cesaro summable to f and a
Tauberian condition is satisfied, then L:-'=  1 f,. converges uniformly to f,"
will have an application to Fourier series. The Tauberian condition is that
{nf,.} is uniformly bounded. The theorem is due to G. H. Hardy.

   For ordinary infinite series, Hardy's theorem takes the following form: If
I::°-1 a,. is Cesaro summable to L and {na,.} is bounded, then I::°-1 a,.
converges to L. Hardy conjectured, but was unable to prove, that if I:,."°..
                                                                         1 a,.
is Abel summable to Land {na,.} is bounded, then I::°-1 a,. converges to L.
This much deeper result was first proved by Hardy's colleague, Littlewood.
Littlewood's theorem was a significant generalization of Tauber's original
theorem (Theorem 65.3) for the Tauberian condition lim,... ao na,. = 0 of
Theorem 65.3 implies that {na,.} is bounded. Littlewood's theorem also
generalizes Hardy's theorem since, as we have pointed out before, Cesaro
summability implies Abel summability. (See Hardy, 1949.)
   Hardy's theorem has a nice application to Fourier series since, as we will
show, the terms of the Fourier series of a function of bounded variation
satisfy Hardy's Tauberian condition.

Theorem 79.2 Let f e BV[a, a + 2nJ. Then the terms of the Fourier series
off are uniformly bounded on R.
  Proof.    First suppose that/ is increasing on [a, a + 2n]. Let
                            a    ao

  We may combine Fejer's theorem (Theorem 77.5) and the results of this

Theorem 79.3 If /e CP[a,a+ 2n] n BV[a, a+ 2n], then the Fourier
series off converges uniformly to f on R.
   Proof. By Theorem 77.5, the Fourier series off is uniformly Cesaro
summable to f on R. By Theorem 79.2, the terms of the Fourier series off
satisfy Hardy's Tauberian condition. By Theorem 79.1, the Fourier series
off converges uniformly to f on R. ■

                                                             XIII
              Normed Linear Spaces and the Riesz
                        Representation Theorem




In this chapter we will present a brief introduction to functional analysis
and then prove the Riesz representation theorem (Theorem 84.1), which
states that if Tis a continuous linear transformation (see Appendix: Vector
Spaces) from C(a, b] into R, then there exists ace BV[a, b] such that T(f) =
I!/ th. for all/ e C[a, b].

80. Normed Linear Spaces and Continuous Linear
    Transformations

Functional analysis can be described as the study of normed linear spaces
(and other more general vector spaces) in terms of the continuous linear
transformations on these spaces.
   We recall (see Section 69) that a normed linear space is a (real) vector
space V together ~th a norm II· II on V. The metric on a normed linear
space is given by
                       d(x,y)   = llx - YII   x,ye V

and metric space concepts applied to V are relative to this metric.
   When dealing with several normed linear spaces and when there is no
possibility of confusion, we follow the usual practice of using the same
notation II•IIto denote any of the norms under consideration. For example,
in Theorem 80.1, Tis a linear transformation from V into W, where V and W
are normed linear spaces. In part (iv) of this theorem we find the inequality
                         UT(x)IIs KUxU        xe V
Obviously, IIT(x)II is the norm in Wand Uxllis the norm in V.

Let V and W be vector spaces. Let !i''(V, W) denote the set of all linear
transformations from V into W. The set !i''(V, W) is itself a vector space if
we define operations pointwise
         (T + UXx) = T(x) + U(x)               (cTXx)    = c(T(x))

respectively, !i'' ( V, W) is identified with the space of all m x n matrices.
  If V and Ware normed linear spaces, we may consider the subset !i'( V, W)
of !i''(V, W) of all continuous linear transformations of V into W. The next
theorem states that !i'( V, W) is a subspace of !i''( V, W).

vector space where the operations are defined pointwise.

83. Introduction to the Riesz Representation Theorem

In Example 80.4 we showed that if ace BV[a, b], then the equation

              (verify). The Riesz representation theorem is the converse of the
above: if Te (C[a, bD*,then there exists aceBV[a, b] such that T(f) = I!f dot.
Moreover, we shall show that IITII = V!ac.      Thus the Riesz representation
theorem states that (C[a, bD* may be identified with BV(a, b]. Linearity is
one of the fundamental properties of the integral (Theorem 53.2). The Riesz
representation theorem shows that linearity and continuity characterize the
Riemann-Stieltjes integral with respect to continuous functions.
   Let Te (C[a, bD*. To prove the Riesz representation theorem, we must
define a function acon [a, b], prove that ace BV[a, b], and finally show that

                                            The Lebesgue Integral




Consider the set S of rational numbers in [0, I]. Since S is countable, we let
S = {a.};>.1 be an enumeration of S. For n = 1, 2, ... , the function
                                for x = a1 with i = 1, 2, 3, ... , n
              f.(x)   = {:
                                for all other x e [0, 1)
is Riemann integrable and



Since 0 S / 1(x) S / 2 (x) S · · · S 1 for all x e [0, 1), we let/(x)   = limn... /.(x)

for each x e [0, 1]. Clearly
                                       if XE S
                      f(x)   = {:      for all other x e [0, I]
It seems reasonable that/ should have integral 0, since each f. has integral 0.
However, since



/ is not even Riemann integrable. This is an inadequacy of the Riemann
and Riemann-Stieltjes integrals, which we now attempt to remedy.
   Why is/ not Riemann integrable? The fault lies not with the average
used, namely cx(x)= x, but rather with the sets we averaged over, i.e., inter-
vals. Using intervals is too restrictive. We now set a new goal. We wish to
carry our concept of integral one more step, so that the class of functions
which we can integrate is much larger than Bt[a, b] and so that the integral

                                                                             366
368                                                      The Lebeague Integral


will not only retain the properties of the Riemann-Stieltjes integral, but will
have additional properties which will allow us to deal with the integral of
the limit of functions.
   To keep the abstract flavor of this integral, we develop it from its rudimen-
tary definitions and finish with examples of this integral on the real line.


86. The Extended Real Line

To accommodate our integration and measure theory, we now extend the
real line R by adjoining two points, denoted co and - co. The order relation
< and the operations of addition and multiplication are also extended in
a natural way, with the exception of 0· co and co - oo. We let 0· oo = 0,
since we wish to ignore what happens on sets of measure zero. However,
co - co remains undefined since situations in which co - co occur con-
stitute major sources of difficulty in measure theory.

DefinitionSS.1
  (i) A= Ru {co, -co}.
For any xeR
 (ii) - co < x < co and - co < co. Also < has its usual meaning on R.
(iii) x + co = co + x = co + co = co and x - co = - co + x = - co -
      co = -co. Also + has its usual meaning on Rand - has its usual
      meaning on R.
(iv) If x > 0, then x·oo = oo·x = oo·oo = (-oo)·(-oo)          = co and x·
      (-co)=   (-oo)·x = oo·(-oo) = (-oo)·oo = -co. · has its usual
      meaning on R.
 (v) If x < 0, then x·oo = oo·x = -co and x·(-oo) = (-oo)·x = co.
(vi) 0·oo = oo·0 = 0·(-oo) = (-oo)·0 = 0.
Therefore, the extended operations + and · are still commutative and
associative, but the distributive law fails in A. For example; oo ·(3 - 2) =
co· 1 = co, but (co· 3) - (co ·2) = co - co is undefined.
   We shall sometimes refer to a function/: x ➔ [-co, co] which takes on
only real values as finite valued.

DeftnltionSS.2 For a, b e A,a < b define
                        [a, b] = {x e A I a s x S b}
                       (a, b]   = {x e A I a < x s b}
                       [a, b) = {x e A I a S x < b}
                       (a, b) = {x e A I a < x < b}
88. u-Algebru and Poaltlve MNsur•                                            367


Any of the above sets is called an interval of I. The open sets in I are the
open sets of I together with sets of the formU v / 1 v / 2 , where U is open in
R and / 1and / 2 are intervals of I of the form [- oo, a) or (a, oo] for a e I.

Example. (-1, 1) v (5, oo] is an open set in I. Also [- oo, oo] = [- oo,
1) v 0 v (0, oo] is an open set in I.

Note.   [- oo, oo] = I. We will use these notations interchangably.

88. o--Algebrasand Positive Measures

Definition86.1 Let X be a set. A collection of subsets JI of Xis said to be
a o--algebra in X if
 (i) 0 E .,I(,

(iii) A. e JI for n = 1, 2, 3, ...   implies U:..1 A. e .,II.

Examples. Let X be any set and recall that 9'(X) denotes the collection of
all subsets of X. It is easily seen that 9'(X) is a o--algebra in X.

  Another o--algebra in X is JI = {0, X}, which also trivially satisfies
Definition 86.1.
  Note that any o--algebra in X always lies between the o--algebras {0, X}
and 9'(X).

88.        Integration on Positive Measure Spaces

We now develop a theory of integration on any positive measure space
(X, ..II, µ). The simplicity of the development and the power of the results
are the real beauty of this part of mathematics.
  In the following X is a set, ..II is a a-algebra in X, and µ is a positive
measure on ..II.
  We begin by defining the integral of a nonnegative simple measurable
function on X.

Definition88.1 Let s be a nonnegative simple measurable function in X,
s = :I:j. 1 c,xA,• where A, e ..II and c1 ~ 0 for i = 1, ... , n. We define the
integral of s over X with respect to µ to be

                                J   X
                                        s dµ =   r,
                                                 l• l
                                                        c,µ(.A 1)


Notice that Ix s dµ ~ 0.
  Consider (P, 9'(P), µ), where µ is counting measure. Supposes = {s11} is a
nonnegative simple function on P. If s = :I:i'..1 c,xA,• where c1 > 0 for
I= 1, ... , m,
                                           M                    ~

                                           L c,µ(A 1) = 11•1
                                                         L s,.
                           J
                           P
                               sdµ =
                                          l•l

This sum is finite if and only if for some N, s,. = 0 for n ~ N.
  The reasoning for our choice of the number Ix s dµ as our integral of s
over Xis clear, since we are averaging s over the set X and weighing the
various disjoint subsets of X, where s takes on different values, by means of
our measureµ. We used a similar method when integrating step functions
over an interval [a, b] with the Riemann integral.
  We use the following definition to extend Definition 88.2 to the class of all
nonnegative measurable functions on X.

   With modest additional hypotheses, a converse of Theorem 88.8 can be
proved. This converse is a special case of a more general result known as the
Lebesgue-Radon-Nikodym Theorem (see Hewitt and Stromberg, 1965,
p. 315). One can show that ifµ and A.are positive measures on the u-algebra
JI satisfying µ(X) < oo and l(X) < oo and further µ(£) = 0 implies l(E) =
0, then there exists a nonnegative measurable function f on X such that

                                            A.(E) = j.Jdµ

for every Ee JI.
  In Section 57 we said that a property P holds almost everywhere if the set
of points on the line where P does not hold is of measure zero. We now
generalize this definition to an arbitrary measure space.

Definition88.9 If a property P holds for every x e E\A and if µ(A) = 0
(where E, A e JI, of course), then we say that P holds for almost all x e E
with respect to µ or that P holds µ-almost everywhere on E or that P holds
µ-a.e. on E.

89.     Lebesgue Measure on R

We have developed an integral for certain measurable functions on any
measure space (X, Jt, µ). We have shown that some very powerful integral-
limit theorems always hold for this integral (Theorems 88.6, 88.13, 88.14,
88.15). What does this have to do with the Riemann integral? The satisfying
fact is that there is a u-algebra .,If on [a, b] and a positive measure m on
.,If such that for all Riemann integrable functions/, we have/ e 9'([a, b],
Jt, m) and

                                     r / dm = Ibf(x) dx
                                  J[o,b)                   G


  However, much more will be true. There will be many more functions than
the Riemann integrable functions which we can integrate.
  For example, returning to the function

                                         for all other x e (0, 1]

m-measurable, Theorem 88.6 tells us/ is m-measurable and

   Since f is not Riemann integrable, we see that we have extended the class
of integrable functions and obtained a much more useful class of integrable
functions.
  We will actually construct a u-algebra of R and a measure m on this
u-algebra such that, in a very natural way, we may restrict m and the u-
algebra to any closed interval [a, b] and get the desired results.

  We have constructed a function m: a'(R)-. [O,oo] with the following
properties:
89.   Lebesgue Measure on A                                               386

  (i) m(Qf) = 0.
 (ii) m(A) s m(B) if A c B c R.
(iii) m(LJ:°-1 AJ s l::°- 1 m(A.J for any sequence {A,.}:°-1 of subsets of R.
    We now return to the abstract setting, because the development of a
a-algebra on which m is countably additive does not depend on R, but only
on the properties stated above.

Definition89.5 Let X be a set. A function µ from 9'(X) into [0, oo] is called
an outer measureif
  (i) µ(Qf) = 0.
 (ii) µ(A) s µ(B) whenever A c B c X.
(iii) µ(LJ:. 1 A.J s l::°- 1 µ(AJ for all sequences {A.,.}:°-
                                                           1 of subsets of X.


  This last property is called the countable subadditivity ofµ. We note that
countable additivity demands the pairwise disjointness of the sets involved,
while countable subadditivity does not.
  The following definition is due to Caratheodory and is the key to defining
a useful a-algebra on which µ will be a positive measure.

Definition89.6 Let X be a set and let µ be an outer measure on 9'(X). A
subset S of X is said to be µ-measurableif
             µ(T) = µ(Sn T) + µ(S' n T)           for all Tc   X
Let JI" denote the family of all µ-measurable subsets of X.

  Since T = (S n T) u (S' n T), it follows from the subadditivity ofµ that
                      µ(T) s µ(Sn T) + µ(S' n T)
Thus the equation in Definition 89.6 holds precisely when
                      µ(T) :.:::µ(S n T) + µ(S' n T)
  A subset S of X is not µ-measurable if there exists a set T c X with
                      µ(T) > µ(S n T) + µ(S' n T)
Notice that in this case µ is not even finitely additive on the disjoint pair
{Sn T, S' n T}. It can be shown (see Hewitt and Stromberg, 1965) that
there exists a non-m-measurable set S c R, where m is given in Definition
89.2. Thus mis not even finitely additive on 9'(R). Again, we see that we must
restrict m to a a-algebra which is properly contained in 9'(R).
  We now proceed to show that JI,, is a a-algebra in X and µ is a positive
measure on JI,,.

                            [a, b] = {S f"'I [a, b] IS e ..If111}

 u-algebra in the set [a, b] (verify). Also since m is a positive measure on .,II,..,
 m is a positive measure on .,II 111[a,b](verify).

 Deflnition90.2 The positive measure space ([a, b],--" ,..[a,b], m) is called
 the Lebesgue measure on [a, b].

   In Section 57 we introduced the concept of a subset of[a, b] having measure
zero. It is now clear from the definition of m that a subset A of [a, b] has
measure zero if and only if A e .,II ,..(a,b] and m(A) = 0 (see Theorem 89.7).
From Section 57 we may therefore conclude that every countable subset of
[a, b] is of m measure zero and the Cantor set in (0, 1) is of m measure zero.
   Recall that a property Pis said to hold almost everywhere on [a, b] if the
set of points where P does not hold has measure zero. Therefore, we have that
m-almost everywhere coincides with the concept of almost everywhere.
   ([a, b], .,II ,..(a,b], m) is a positive measure space; so by Section 88 we have
an integral Jc0 ,,, 1/ dm define" on a subclass of the class of all .,II ,..(a,b]-
measurable functions on [a, b]. Thus the theorems of Section 88 are valid for
this integral. We call this integral the Lebesgue integral on [a, b], and we call
the functions in 9'([a, b], .,II111(a,      b], m) (Definition 88.3), the Lebesgue
integrable functions on [a, b], denoted 9'(a, b].
   We now fill in the important link between the Riemann integral and the
Lebesgue integral on [a, b].

                                       Appendix: Vector Spaces




In this brief appendix we summarize some of the key definitions and theorems
concerning vector spaces which are used in this text.

Definition. Let F be a field. A vector space V over a field Fis the pair ( V, F)
together with operations + : V x V ➔ V and · : F x V ➔ V satisfying
l. x + y = y + x for all x, ye V.
2. x + (y + z) = (x + y) + z for all x, y, z e V.
3. There exists (J e V such that x + (J = x for all x e V.
4. For each x e V, there exists ye V such that x + y = 0.
5. c(dx) = (cd)x for all c, de F; x e V.
6. lx = x for all x e V, where 1 is the multiplicative identity for F.
1. c(x + y) = ex+ cy for all c e F; x, ye V.
8. (c + d)x = ex + dx for all c, de F; x e V.
  We call V the set of vectors and the operation +: V x V ➔ V vector
addition. We call F the set of scalars and the operation ·: F x V ➔ V scalar
multiplication. When the field Fis understood, we speak of the vector space
 V. We also note that a vector space Vis always nonempty since (J e V. In
case F = R, we call V a real vector space. Throughout the remainder of
this appendix we shall assume that all vector spaces are real vector spaces.
   If X is a set and $Tix is the set of real-valued functions on X, then $Tix is a
vector space ifwe define vector addition and scalar multiplication pointwise:
                (f + gXx) = f(x)       + g(x)   f,g e $Tix; x e X
                    (cfXx)   = cf(x)
Most of the vector spaces considered in this text are subsets of,     x for some

                                                                        406
408                                                              Appendix: Vector Spaces


set X. For example, if we let X = {l, 2, ... , n},, xis the vector space R•.
It can be shown that any (real) vector space is essentially a subset of , x
for some set X.

Definition. Let V be a vector space and X c V. We say that X is linearly
independent if whenever {x 1 , ••• , x 11} is a finite subset of X and for scalars
c 1 , ••• , c11 we have c1x 1 + · · · + c,,x11 = 8, it follows that
                                   0 = c 1 = Cz = · · •= C•
  The set X is linearly dependent if it is not linearly independent, that is, if
there exists a finite subset {xi, ... , x11} of X and scalars Ci, ••• , c., not all
zero, such that c1x 1 + · · · + c,,x11 = 8.

Definition. Let W, X c V. If for every clement w e W, there exist x 1 , •••                ,
x 11 e X and c., ... , c11 e R such that


we say that X spans W.

Definition. A subset X c V is a basis for V if X is a linearly independent
set and X spans V.

'Theorem. A subset X c Vis a basis for V if and only if every element v e V
may be written uniquely as
                                   V   = C 1X1 + •••+ C,,X  11


where c 1 , •••   ,   c11 e Rand x 1 , •••   ,   x,. e X.

Definition. A vector space Vis finite dimensional if V = {0} or if there exists
a basis X for V, where X is a finite set.

Theorem. If Vis a finite dimensional vector space and V =I:{8}, then every
basis for Vis finite and any two bases have the same number of elements.

Definition. If V is a finite dimensional vector space, V =I:{ 0}, and X is a
basis for V, we define the dimension of V to be the number of elements in X.
If V = {8}, we define the dimension of V to be zero.

Theorem.      The (vector space) dimension of R"is n.

Theorem. Let Xi, ••• , x,. e R".The following are equivalent.
 (i) {xi, ... , x,.} is a basis for R".

 (ii) { x., . . . , x.} spans R•.
(iii) {x1 , ••• , x.} is a linearly independent set.

Deflnition. If W c V, we say that W is a subspace of V if W itself is a
vector space.

Theorem. W is a subspace of V if and only if whenever x, y e Wand c e R,
x + eye W.
  Throughout the remainder of this Appendix Vi and V2 will denote real
vector spaces.

Definition. A linear transformation Tfrom Vi to V2 is a function T: Vi ➔     V2
satisfying T(x + cy) = (Tx) + c(Ty) for x, ye V1 and c e R.

Definition. A linear functional on Vi is a linear transformation from Vi to
R, where R is considered a vector space over itself.

Deftnltlon.   Let !11'(Vi, V2) denote the set of linear transformations from
V1 to V2 • If T, U e !i1'(V., V2), c e R, and v e V., we define
                          (T + UXv) = (Tv) + (Uv)
                              (cTXv) = c(Tv)

Theorem. !i1'(Vi, V2) with vector addition and scalar multiplication defined
as above is a real vector space.

Theorem. If Te !i1'(Vi, V2 ) and Se !i1'(V2 , V3 ), then the composition
S O Te !i1'(V., V3). (Of course, V3 denotes a real vector space.)
  The composition S O T is ordinarily written ST.

Definition. If Te !i1'(Vi, V2 ) and Tis one to one and onto, we call Ta
(vector space) isomorphism and we say that V1 and V2 are isomorphic (vector
spaces).

Theorem. Let V be a vector space. V is isomorphic to R• if and only if V
is n-dimensional.

Definition. If Te !i1'(V1 , V2 ) and there exists Se !i1'(V2 , Vi) such that TS
is the identity on V2 and ST is the identity on V1 , we say that Tis invertible.
In this case we denote S by T- 1•

such that T = s- 1 us, we say that Tand Uaresimilarlineartransformations.
